				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Branch of Sino-Tibetan languages</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic languages</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the sociolinguistic treatment of Chinese, see <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">Varieties of Chinese</a>. For other languages spoken in China, see <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_China" title="Languages of China">Languages of China</a>.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox" style="width:22em;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="background-color: salmon;">Sinitic</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-subheader" style="background-color: salmon;">Chinese</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Ethnicity</th><td class="infobox-data">Sinitic peoples</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Geographic<br />distribution</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Island" title="Christmas Island">Christmas Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_diaspora" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese diaspora">Chinese diaspora</a> communities, <a href="/wiki/Ferghana_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferghana Valley">Ferghana Valley</a> (Uzbekistan) and <a href="/wiki/Chu_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Chu River">Chu Valley</a> (Kyrgyzstan) (<a href="/wiki/Dungan_people" title="Dungan people">Dungans</a>), Russian Far East (<a href="/wiki/Taz_people" title="Taz people">Tazs</a>)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">Linguistic classification</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan</a><ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:15px;padding-left:0"><li><b>Sinitic</b></li></ul></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Proto-language</th><td class="infobox-data">Proto-Sinitic</td></tr><tr class="plainlist"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Subdivisions</th><td class="infobox-data">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Macro-Bai_languages" title="Macro-Bai languages">Macro-Bai</a> (?)</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-5" title="ISO 639-5">ISO 639-5</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><samp><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/zhx" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:zhx">zhx</a></samp></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><samp><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/sini1245">sini1245</a></samp>&#160; <span class="nowrap">(Sinitic)</span><br /><samp><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/macr1275">macr1275</a></samp>&#160; <span class="nowrap">(Macro-Bai)</span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/300px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="238" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/450px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/600px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2219" data-file-height="1759" /></a></span><div style="text-align:left;">Map of Sinitic languages in China</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Sinitic languages</b><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans">汉语族</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant">漢語族</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Hànyǔ zú</span></i>), often synonymous with the <b>Chinese languages</b>, are a <a href="/wiki/Language_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Language group">group</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic languages</a> that constitute a major branch of the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan language family</a>. It is frequently proposed that there is a primary split between the Sinitic languages and the rest of the family (the <a href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman_languages" title="Tibeto-Burman languages">Tibeto-Burman languages</a>). This view is rejected by a number of researchers<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Driem2001351_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Driem2001351-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> but has found phylogenetic support among others.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESagartJacquesLaiRyder2019_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESagartJacquesLaiRyder2019-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Macro-Bai_languages" title="Macro-Bai languages">Macro-Bai languages</a>, whose classification is difficult, may be an offshoot of <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> and thus Sinitic;<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> otherwise Sinitic is defined only by the many <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a> unified by a shared historical background, and usage of the term "Sinitic" may reflect the linguistic view that <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> constitutes a family of distinct languages, rather than variants of a single language.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Population"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Over 91% of the Chinese population speaks a Sinitic language.<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> A total of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7009152194370000000♠"></span>1<span style="margin-left:.25em;">521</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">943</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">700</span></span> people are speakers of the Chinese macrolanguage, of whom about three-quarters speak a Mandarin variety. Estimates of the number of global speakers of Sinitic branches as of 2018–19, both native and non-native, are listed below:<sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Languages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_varieties_of_Chinese" title="List of varieties of Chinese">List of varieties of Chinese</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:ChineseInST.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/ChineseInST.svg/220px-ChineseInST.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/ChineseInST.svg/330px-ChineseInST.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/ChineseInST.svg/440px-ChineseInST.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="330" /></a><figcaption>L1 speakers of Chinese and other Sino-Tibetan languages according to <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i></figcaption></figure>
<p>Dialectologist <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Norman_(sinologist)" title="Jerry Norman (sinologist)">Jerry Norman</a> estimated that there are hundreds of mutually unintelligible Sinitic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman200372_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman200372-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  They form a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a> in which differences generally become more pronounced as distances increase, though there are also some sharp boundaries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988189–190_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988189–190-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The Sinitic languages can be divided into Macro-Bai languages and Chinese languages, and the following is one of many potential ways of subdividing these languages. Some varieties, such as <a href="/wiki/Shaozhou_Tuhua" title="Shaozhou Tuhua">Shaozhou Tuhua</a>, are hard to classify, and thus are not included in the following briefs.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macro-Bai_languages">Macro-Bai languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Macro-Bai languages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This is a language family first proposed by linguist <a href="/wiki/Zhengzhang_Shangfang" title="Zhengzhang Shangfang">Zhengzhang Shangfang</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and was expanded to include Longjia and Luren.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> It likely split off from the rest of Sinitic during the <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The languages included are all considered minority languages in China and are spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwest China">Southwest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The languages are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bai_language" title="Bai language">Bai</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cai-Long_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Cai-Long languages">Cai-Long</a>: <a href="/wiki/Caijia" class="mw-redirect" title="Caijia">Caijia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Longjia_language" title="Longjia language">Longjia</a><abbr title="Extinct" aria-label="Extinct" style="border: none; text-decoration: none; cursor: inherit; font-weight: normal; font-style: normal;">†</abbr>, <a href="/wiki/Luren_language" title="Luren language">Luren</a><abbr title="Extinct" aria-label="Extinct" style="border: none; text-decoration: none; cursor: inherit; font-weight: normal; font-style: normal;">†</abbr></li></ul>
<p>All other Sinitic languages henceforth would be considered Chinese.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese">Chinese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chinese"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Chinese branch of the family is classified into at least seven main families. These families are classified based on five main evolutionary criteria:<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The evolution of the historical fully muddy (<span lang="zh-Hans">全浊</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">全濁</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">quánzhuó</span></i>) initials</li>
<li>The distribution of rimes across the four tone qualities, as conditioned by voicing and aspiration of initials</li>
<li>The evolution of the checked (<span lang="zh">入</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">rù</span></i>) tone category</li>
<li>The loss or retention of coda position plosives and nasals</li>
<li>The palatalisation of the <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">jiàn</i></span> initial (<span lang="zh">見母</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">jiànmǔ</span></i>) in front of high vowels</li></ol>
<p>The varieties within one family may not be mutually intelligible with each other. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Wenzhou dialect">Wenzhounese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ningbo_dialect" title="Ningbo dialect">Ningbonese</a> are not highly mutually intelligible. The <a href="/wiki/Language_Atlas_of_China" title="Language Atlas of China">Language Atlas of China</a> identifies ten groups:<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Huizhou_Chinese" title="Huizhou Chinese">Hui</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">Xiang</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pinghua_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinghua Chinese">Pinghua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shaozhou_Tuhua" title="Shaozhou Tuhua">Tuhua</a></li></ul>
<p>with Jin, Hui, Pinghua, and Tuhua not part of the seven traditional groups.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mandarin">Mandarin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mandarin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Varieties of Mandarin are used in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Western Regions</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwest China">Southwest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huguang" title="Huguang">Huguang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhongyuan" title="Zhongyuan">Central Plains</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_China" title="Northeast China">Northeast</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> by around three-quarters of the Sinitic-speaking population.<sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Historically, the prestige variety has always been Mandarin, which is still reflected to this day in <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-diversity_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In fact, Standard Chinese is now an official language of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Re-population efforts, such as that of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a> in the Southwest, tended to involve Mandarin speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Classification of Mandarin lects has undergone several significant changes, though nowadays it is commonly divided as such, based on the distribution of the historical checked tone:<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Mandarin" title="Northeastern Mandarin">Northeastern</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Beijing_Mandarin_(division_of_Mandarin)" title="Beijing Mandarin (division of Mandarin)">Beijing</a> (sometimes considered part of Northeastern)<sup id="cite_ref-ne_beijing_1_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ne_beijing_1-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ne_beijing_2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ne_beijing_2-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jiao-Liao_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Jiao-Liao Mandarin">Jiaoliao</a> (sometimes "Peninsular")</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ji-Lu_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Ji-Lu Mandarin">Jilu</a> (sometimes "Northern")</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Central_Plains_Mandarin" title="Central Plains Mandarin">Central Plains</a> (or "Zhongyuan")</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lan-Yin_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Lan-Yin Mandarin">Lanyin</a> (sometimes "Northwestern" and considered part of Central Plains)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a> (often considered its own top-level group due to the <a href="/wiki/Language_Atlas_of_China" title="Language Atlas of China">Language Atlas of China</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Mandarin" title="Southwestern Mandarin">Southwestern</a> (sometimes "Upper Yangtse")</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jianghuai_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Jianghuai Mandarin">Jianghuai</a> (or "Lower Yangtze", sometimes "Huai", "Southern" or "Southeastern")<sup id="cite_ref-diversity_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>as well as other lects, which do not neatly fall into these categories, such as Mandarin <a href="/wiki/Junhua" class="mw-redirect" title="Junhua">Junhua</a> varieties.
</p><p>Varieties of Mandarin can be defined by their universally lost -m final, low number of tones, and smaller inventory of <a href="/wiki/Classifier_(linguistics)" title="Classifier (linguistics)">classifiers</a>, among other features. Mandarin lects also often have rhotic <a href="/wiki/Erhua" title="Erhua">erhua</a> rimes, though the amount of its use may vary between lects.<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Loss of checked tone is an often cited criterion for Mandarin languages, though lects such as Yangzhounese and Taiyuannese show otherwise.
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northeastern_and_Beijing_Mandarin">Northeastern and Beijing Mandarin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Northeastern and Beijing Mandarin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Northeastern Mandarin is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Heilongjiang" title="Heilongjiang">Heilongjiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jilin" title="Jilin">Jilin</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Liaoning" title="Liaoning">Liaoning</a> and northeastern <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>, whereas Beijing Mandarin is spoken in northern <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The two families' most notable features are the heavy use of <a href="/wiki/Rhotic_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhotic vowel">rhotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Erhua" title="Erhua">erhua</a> and seemingly random distribution of the dark checked tone, and generally having four tones with the contours of high flat, rising, dipping, and falling.
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">

<p>Northeastern Mandarin, especially in Heilongjiang, contains many loanwords from Russian.<sup id="cite_ref-harbin_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harbin-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Term</th>
<th>Pronunciation</th>
<th>Meaning</th>
<th>Origin
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="zh">卜留克</span></td>
<td><i><span lang="zh-Latn">bǔliúkè</span></i></td>
<td>'<a href="/wiki/Rutabaga" title="Rutabaga">rutabaga</a>'</td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">брюква</span></span> <i>bryukva</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="zh">馬神</span></td>

<td>'machine'</td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">машина</span></span> <i>mashina</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="zh">巴籬子</span></td>
<td><i><span lang="zh-Latn">bālízi</span></i></td>
<td>'jail'</td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">полиция</span></span> <i>politsiya</i>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Northeastern Mandarin lects can be divided into three main groups, namely Hafu (including <a href="/wiki/Harbin_dialect" title="Harbin dialect">Harbinnese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Changchun_dialect" title="Changchun dialect">Changchunnese</a>), Jishen (including <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jilin_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jilin dialect (page does not exist)">Jilinnese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shenyang_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Shenyang dialect">Shenyangnese</a>), and Heisong. Notably, the extinct <a href="/wiki/Taz_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Taz language">Taz language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> is also a Northeastern Mandarin language. Beijing is sometimes included in Northeastern Mandarin due to its distribution of the historical dark checked tone,<sup id="cite_ref-ne_beijing_1_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ne_beijing_1-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ne_beijing_2_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ne_beijing_2-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> though is listed as its own group by others, often due to its more regular light checked tones.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jilu_Mandarin">Jilu Mandarin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Jilu Mandarin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Jilu Mandarin is spoken in southern Hebei and western <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and is often represented with <a href="/wiki/Jinan_dialect" title="Jinan dialect">Jinannese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dict_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Notable cities that use Jilu Mandarin lects include <a href="/wiki/Cangzhou" title="Cangzhou">Cangzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shijiazhuang" title="Shijiazhuang">Shijiazhuang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jinan" title="Jinan">Jinan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baoding" title="Baoding">Baoding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Characteristically Jilu Mandarin features include merging the dark checked into the dark level tone, the light checked into light level or departing based on the <a href="/wiki/Manner_of_articulation" title="Manner of articulation">manner of articulation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Initial_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Initial (linguistics)">initial</a>, and vowel breaking in <i>tong</i> rime series' (<span lang="zh">通攝</span>) checked-tone words, among other features.
</p><p>Jilu Mandarin can be classified into Baotang, Shiji, Canghui and Zhangli.<sup id="cite_ref-mandarin_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mandarin-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Zhangli is of note due to its preservation of a separate checked tone.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jiaoliao_Mandarin">Jiaoliao Mandarin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Jiaoliao Mandarin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Jiaoliao.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Jiaoliao.png/220px-Jiaoliao.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="345" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Jiaoliao.png/330px-Jiaoliao.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Jiaoliao.png/440px-Jiaoliao.png 2x" data-file-width="1394" data-file-height="2187" /></a><figcaption>Distribution of Jiaoliao Mandarin varieties</figcaption></figure>
<p>Jiaoliao Mandarin is spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Shandong_Peninsula" title="Shandong Peninsula">Jiaodong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">Liaodong Peninsulae</a>, which includes the cities of <a href="/wiki/Dalian" title="Dalian">Dalian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qingdao" title="Qingdao">Qingdao</a>, as well as several prefectures along the China-Korea border.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Like Jilu Mandarin, its light checked tone is merged into light level or departing based on the manner of articulation of the initial, though its dark checked is merged into the rising. Its <i><span lang="zh-Latn">rì</span></i> initial (<span lang="zh">日母</span>) terms are pronounced with a <a href="/wiki/Null_initial" class="mw-redirect" title="Null initial">null initial</a> (apart from open <i><span lang="zh-Latn">zhǐ</span></i> rime series (<span lang="zh">止攝開口</span>) finals), unlike the <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʐ/</span> of Northern and Beijing Mandarin.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on, for example, the pronunciation of the <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalized</a> <i><span lang="zh-Latn">jiàn</span></i> initial (<span lang="zh">見母</span>),<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Jiaoliao Mandarin can be divided into Qingzhou, Denglian and Gaihuan areas.<sup id="cite_ref-mandarin_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mandarin-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Plains_and_Lanyin_Mandarin">Central Plains and Lanyin Mandarin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Central Plains and Lanyin Mandarin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Central Plains Mandarin is spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Zhongyuan" title="Zhongyuan">Central Plains</a> of <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a>, southwestern <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, as well as most of <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Ningxia" title="Ningxia">Ningxia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, in famous cities such as <a href="/wiki/Kaifeng" title="Kaifeng">Kaifeng</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhengzhou" title="Zhengzhou">Zhengzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xuzhou" title="Xuzhou">Xuzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi&#39;an">Xi'an</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xining" title="Xining">Xining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lanzhou" title="Lanzhou">Lanzhou</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Central Plains Mandarin lects merge the historical checked tones with a lesser muddy (<span lang="zh">次濁</span>) and clear (<span lang="zh">清</span>) initial together with the rising tone, and those with a fully muddy (<span lang="zh">全濁</span>) initial are merged with the light level tone.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lanyin Mandarin, spoken in northern Ningxia, parts of Gansu and northern Xinjiang, is sometimes grouped together with Central Plains Mandarin due to its merged lesser light and dark checked tones, though it is realised as a departing tone.
</p><p>Subdivision of Central Plains Mandarin is not fully agreed upon, though one possible subdivision sees 13 divisions, namely Xuhuai, Zhengkai, Luosong, Nanlu, Yanhe, Shangfu, Xinbeng, Luoxiang, Fenhe, Guanzhong, Qinlong, Longzhong and Nanjiang.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Lanyin Mandarin, on the other hand, is divided as Jincheng, Yinwu, Hexi and Beijiang. The <a href="/wiki/Dungan_language" title="Dungan language">Dungan language</a> is a collection of Central Plains Mandarin varieties spoken in the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jin">Jin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Jin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Jinyufen_qu.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Jinyufen_qu.png/220px-Jinyufen_qu.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="278" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Jinyufen_qu.png/330px-Jinyufen_qu.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Jinyufen_qu.png/440px-Jinyufen_qu.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="646" /></a><figcaption>Distribution of Jin varieties</figcaption></figure>
<p>Jin is spoken in most of <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> and central <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> often represented by <a href="/wiki/Taiyuan_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Taiyuan dialect">Taiyuannese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dict_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> It was first proposed as a lect separate from the rest of Mandarin by <a href="/wiki/Li_Rong_(linguist)" title="Li Rong (linguist)">Li Rong</a>, where it was proposed as lects in and around Shanxi with a checked tone, though this stance is not without disagreement.<sup id="cite_ref-gailun_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gailun-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Jin varieties also often has disyllabic words derived from syllable splitting (分音詞), through the infixation of <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/(u)əʔ<span class="wrap"> </span>l/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">笨</p><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">pəŋ꜄</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">&#160;</p><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">→</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">薄</p><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">pəʔ꜇</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">愣</p><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ləŋ꜄</p></div><p style="display: none;">笨 {} 薄 愣</p><p style="display: none;">pəŋ꜄ → pəʔ꜇ ləŋ꜄</p><p style="clear: left;">'stupid'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">滾</p><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">꜂kʊŋ</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">&#160;</p><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">→</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">骨</p><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">kuəʔ꜆</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">攏</p><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">꜂lʊŋ</p></div><p style="display: none;">滾 {} 骨 攏</p><p style="display: none;">꜂kʊŋ → kuəʔ꜆ ꜂lʊŋ</p><p style="clear: left;">'to roll'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>As per the Language Atlas by Li, Jin is divided into Dabao, Zhanghu, Wutai, Lüliang, Bingzhou, Shangdang, Hanxin, and Zhiyan branches.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southwestern_Mandarin">Southwestern Mandarin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Southwestern Mandarin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Spoken in <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a> and bordering parts of <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Kokang" title="Kokang">Kokang</a> of Myanmar and parts of northern <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, Southwestern Mandarin speakers take up the most area and population of all Mandarinic language groups, and would be the eighth most spoken language in the world if separated from the rest of Mandarin.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Southwestern Mandarinic tends to not have <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">retroflex consonants</a>, and merges all checked tone categories together. With the exception of <a href="/wiki/Minjiang_dialect" title="Minjiang dialect">Minchi</a>, which has a standalone checked category, the checked tone is merged with another category. Representative lects include <a href="/wiki/Wuhan_dialect" title="Wuhan dialect">Wuhannese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sichuanese_(language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sichuanese (language)">Sichuanese</a>, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Kunming_dialect" title="Kunming dialect">Kunmingnese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dict_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Southwestern Mandarin tends to be split as Chuanqian, Xishu, Chuanxi, Yunnan, Huguang and Guiliu branches. Minchi is sometimes separated out as a remnant of Old Shu.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Huai">Huai</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Huai"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dialect_Map_of_Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Dialect_Map_of_Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin.png/220px-Dialect_Map_of_Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Dialect_Map_of_Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin.png/330px-Dialect_Map_of_Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Dialect_Map_of_Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin.png/440px-Dialect_Map_of_Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin.png 2x" data-file-width="16001" data-file-height="16001" /></a><figcaption>Distribution of Huai varieties</figcaption></figure>
<p>Huai is spoken in central <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, far western and eastern <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a> and most of <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Due to its preservation of a checked tone, some linguists believe that Huai ought to be treated as a top-level group, like Jin. Representative lects tend to be <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_dialect" title="Nanjing dialect">Nanjingnese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hefei_dialect" title="Hefei dialect">Hefeinese</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangzhou_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Yangzhou dialect (page does not exist)">Yangzhounese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dict_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The Huai of Nanjing has likely served as a national prestige during the Ming and Qing periods,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> though this viewpoint is not supported by all linguists.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Language Atlas divides Huai into Tongtai, Huangxiao, and Hongchao areas, with the latter further split into Ninglu and Huaiyang. Tongtai, being geographically located furthest west, has the most significant Wu influence, such as in its distribution of historical voiced plosive series.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hangzhou_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hangzhou-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">piaŋ</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yue">Yue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Yue"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg/330px-Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="208" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg/495px-Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg/660px-Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="323" /></a><figcaption>Distribution of Yue varieties (including Pinghua)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Yue Chinese is spoken by around 84 million people,<sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> in western <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Hainan_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Hainan province">Hainan</a>, as well as overseas communities such as <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Famous lects such as <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taishan_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Taishan dialect">Taishanese</a> belong to this family.<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Yue Chinese lects generally possess <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">long-short distinctions</a> in their vowels, which is reflected in their almost universally split dark checked and often split light checked tones. They generally also tend to preserve all three checked plosive finals and three nasal finals. The status of Pinghua is uncertain, and some believe its two groups, Northern and Southern, should be listed under Yue,<sup id="cite_ref-guangxi_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guangxi-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> though this standpoint is rejected by some.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Yue is generally split into <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> (which itself contains <a href="/wiki/Yuehai_Yue" title="Yuehai Yue">Yuehai</a>, Xiangshan, and <a href="/wiki/Guan-Bao_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Guan-Bao dialect">Guanbao</a>), <a href="/wiki/Siyi_Yue" title="Siyi Yue">Siyi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaoyang_Yue" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaoyang Yue">Gaoyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qin-Lian_Yue" class="mw-redirect" title="Qin-Lian Yue">Qinlian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wu-Hua_Yue" class="mw-redirect" title="Wu-Hua Yue">Wuhua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goulou_Yue" title="Goulou Yue">Goulou</a> (which includes <a href="/wiki/Luo-Guang_Yue" class="mw-redirect" title="Luo-Guang Yue">Luoguang</a>), <a href="/wiki/Yong-Xun_Yue" class="mw-redirect" title="Yong-Xun Yue">Yongxun</a> and the two Pinghua branches.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Siyi is generally agreed to be the most divergent, and Goulou is believed to be the one which is closest related to Pinghua.<sup id="cite_ref-guangxi_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guangxi-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hakka">Hakka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Hakka"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hakka Chinese is a direct result of several migration waves from Northern China to the South,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and is spoken in eastern <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, as well as scattered points in the rest of Guangdong, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hainan_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Hainan province">Hainan</a>, along with overseas communities such as in <a href="/wiki/West_Kalimantan" title="West Kalimantan">West Kalimantan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangka_Belitung_Islands" title="Bangka Belitung Islands">Bangka Belitung Islands</a> in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, by an estimated total of 44 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Some believe that Hakka is closely related to other groups, such as Gan, Yue, or Tongtai.<sup id="cite_ref-gan_hakka_1_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gan_hakka_1-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gan_hakka_2_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gan_hakka_2-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Hakka varieties generally have no voiced plosive initials and preserve the historical <i><span lang="zh-Latn">rì</span></i> initial (<span lang="zh">日母</span>) as an n-like sound.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-meixian_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meixian-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">

<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><sup>ŋ</sup>ɡit</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">nie</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Hakka can be divided into Yuetai, Hailu, Yuebei, Yuexi, Tingzhou, Ninglong, Yuxin and Tonggui.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Meixian_dialect" title="Meixian dialect">Meizhounese</a> is often used as the representative variety of Hakka.<sup id="cite_ref-dict_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Min">Min</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Min"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Min_Languages.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Min_Languages.svg/220px-Min_Languages.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="255" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Min_Languages.svg/330px-Min_Languages.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Min_Languages.svg/440px-Min_Languages.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1550" data-file-height="1800" /></a><figcaption>Distribution of Min varieties in Mainland China, Hainan and Taiwan</figcaption></figure>
<p>Min Chinese is a direct descendant of Old Chinese, and is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Chaoshan" title="Chaoshan">Chaoshan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhanjiang" title="Zhanjiang">Zhanjiang</a> of <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>, by around 76 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Due to significant amounts of migration, many people in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> are also able of speaking Min varieties. Lects such as <a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teoswa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hainanese" title="Hainanese">Hainanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> (incl. <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Fuzhou_dialect" title="Fuzhou dialect">Hokchiu</a> are all Min varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the fact that Min descended from Old Chinese rather than Middle Chinese, it has some features that would be out of place in other varieties. For instance, some words with the <i><span lang="zh-Latn">cheng</span></i> initial (<span lang="zh">澄母</span>) are not affricates in Min. This, interestingly, has led to many languages, such as <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">Occitan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inuktitut_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Inuktitut language">Inuktitut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maori_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Maori language">Māori</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telugu_language" title="Telugu language">Telugu</a>, loaning the Sinitic word for 'tea' (<span lang="zh">茶</span>) with a plosive. Min varieties also have a very large number of words with <a href="/wiki/Literary_and_colloquial_readings_of_Chinese_characters" title="Literary and colloquial readings of Chinese characters">literary pronunciations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">

<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">tsən</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Min can primarily be split into Coastal and Inland Min varieties. The former contains the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a> branches of Quanzhang (Hokkien), Chaoshan (Teoswa), <a href="/wiki/Datian_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Datian dialect">Datian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhongshan_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhongshan dialect">Zhongshan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Min" title="Eastern Min">Eastern Min</a> branches of Houguan and Funing, Qionglei Min, as well as <a href="/wiki/Puxian_Min" class="mw-redirect" title="Puxian Min">Puxian Min</a>, whereas the latter includes <a href="/wiki/Northern_Min" title="Northern Min">Northern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Min" title="Central Min">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shaojiang_Min" class="mw-redirect" title="Shaojiang Min">Shaojiang Min</a>. Shaojiang Min acts as a translitional area between Min, Gan, and Hakka.<sup id="cite_ref-diversity_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gailun_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gailun-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wu">Wu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Wu"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Varieties_of_of_the_Wu_Chinese_(English).png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Varieties_of_of_the_Wu_Chinese_%28English%29.png/220px-Varieties_of_of_the_Wu_Chinese_%28English%29.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Varieties_of_of_the_Wu_Chinese_%28English%29.png/330px-Varieties_of_of_the_Wu_Chinese_%28English%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Varieties_of_of_the_Wu_Chinese_%28English%29.png/440px-Varieties_of_of_the_Wu_Chinese_%28English%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="2400" /></a><figcaption>Distribution of Wu varieties</figcaption></figure>
<p>Wu Chinese is spoken in most of <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, parts of southern <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> by around 82 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wu_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wu-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Many large cities in the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_Delta" title="Yangtze Delta">Yangtze Delta</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changzhou" title="Changzhou">Changzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ningbo" title="Ningbo">Ningbo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>, use a Wu variety. Wu varieties generally have a fricative initial in their negators, a three-way plosive distinction, as well as a checked coda preserved as a <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a>, with the exception of Oujiang lects, where it has become <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">vowel length</a>, and Xuanzhou.<sup id="cite_ref-wu_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wu-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hangzhou_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hangzhou-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhounese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Wenzhou dialect">Wenzhounese</a> are usually used as representatives of Wu.<sup id="cite_ref-dict_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Wu Chinese varieties generally have a massive number of vowels, which rivals even <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shanghai_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shanghai-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-suzhou_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suzhou-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Jinhui_dialect" title="Jinhui dialect">Dondac variety</a> has been observed to have 20 phonemic monophthongal vowels, according to one analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Qian_Nairong" title="Qian Nairong">Qian Nairong</a> divides Wu into <a href="/wiki/Taihu_Wu" title="Taihu Wu">Taihu</a> (or Northern Wu), <a href="/wiki/Taizhou_dialect" title="Taizhou dialect">Taizhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oujiang_Wu" class="mw-redirect" title="Oujiang Wu">Oujiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chuqu_Wu" class="mw-redirect" title="Chuqu Wu">Chuqu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jinhua_dialect" title="Jinhua dialect">Wuzhou</a>. Northern Wu is further divided into Piling, Suhujia, Tiaoxi, Linshao, Yongjiang and Hangzhou, though Hangzhou's classification is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-hangzhou_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hangzhou-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wu_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wu-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hui">Hui</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hui"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huizhou Chinese is spoken in western <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Jingdezhen" title="Jingdezhen">Jingdezhen</a>, by around 5 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It is identified as a top level group by the Language Atlas, though some linguists believe in other theories, such as it being a Gan-influenced Wu variety, due to an identifiable basis of Old Wu features.<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Hui varieties are phonologically diverse, and some features are shared with Wu, such as the simplification of diphthongs.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Hui can be divided into Jishe, Xiuyi, Qiwu, Jingzhan and Yanzhou branches, with Tunxinese and Jixinese being representatives.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gan">Gan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Gan"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gan Chinese is spoken in northern and central <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>, by 22 million people,<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> sometimes believed to be related to Hakka.<sup id="cite_ref-gan_hakka_1_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gan_hakka_1-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gan_hakka_2_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gan_hakka_2-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Gan varieties tend to not <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalize</a> terms with the <i><span lang="zh-Latn">jian</span></i> initial (<span lang="zh">見母</span>) and have an f-like initial in closed <i><span lang="zh-Latn">xiao</span></i> and <i><span lang="zh-Latn">xia</span></i> initial (<span lang="zh">合口曉匣兩母</span>) terms, among other features.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">
<caption>Pronunciation of terms with a <i><span lang="zh-Latn">xia</span></i> or <span lang="zh">xiao</span> initial and closed medial in Gan

<tbody><tr>

<p>Gan can also be divided into Northern and Southern groups. The Northern group was formed during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a>, whereas the Southern group was developed on the basis of Northern Gan.<sup id="cite_ref-other_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The Language Atlas sees Gan divided into Changdu, Yiliu, Jicha, Fuguang, Yingyi, Datong, Dongsui, Huaiyue and Leizi branches.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nanchang_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanchang dialect">Nanchangnese</a> is often chosen as the representative.<sup id="cite_ref-dict_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Shaojiang Min is identified to be influenced or even closely related to Fuguang Gan.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Xiang">Xiang</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Xiang"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Classification_of_Xiang_2005_Bao.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Classification_of_Xiang_2005_Bao.png/220px-Classification_of_Xiang_2005_Bao.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Classification_of_Xiang_2005_Bao.png/330px-Classification_of_Xiang_2005_Bao.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Classification_of_Xiang_2005_Bao.png/440px-Classification_of_Xiang_2005_Bao.png 2x" data-file-width="860" data-file-height="860" /></a><figcaption>Distribution of Xiang varieties in Hunan and Guangxi</figcaption></figure>
<p>Xiang Chinese is spoken in central and western <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> and nearby parts of <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a> by an estimated 37 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue_china_12-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue_china-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Due to migrations, Xiang can be split into New and Old Xiang groups, with Old Xiang having fewer Mandarin-influenced features.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-other_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-other-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Xiang varieties have universally lost their checked codas, but the majority of them still have a unique preserved checked tone contour. Most also have a three-way plosive distinction, like Wu varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One way of dividing Xiang varieties sees five distinct families, namely Changyi, Hengzhou, Louzhao, Chenxu, and Yongzhou.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Changsha_dialect" title="Changsha dialect">Changshanese</a> and one of <a href="/wiki/Shuangfeng_dialect" title="Shuangfeng dialect">Shuangfengnese</a> or <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loudi_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Loudi dialect (page does not exist)">Loudinese</a> are usually taken as Xiang representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-dict_27-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_classification">Internal classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Internal classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png/220px-The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png/330px-The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png/440px-The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png 2x" data-file-width="2851" data-file-height="1910" /></a><figcaption>After applying the linguistic comparative method to the database of comparative linguistic data developed by <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Sagart" title="Laurent Sagart">Laurent Sagart</a> in 2019 to identify sound correspondences and establish cognates, phylogenetic methods are used to infer relationships among these languages and estimate the age of their origin and homeland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESagartJacquesLaiRyder201910319–10320_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESagartJacquesLaiRyder201910319–10320-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>The traditional, dialectological classification of Chinese languages is based on the evolution of the sound categories of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>. Little comparative work has been done (the usual way of reconstructing the relationships between languages), and little is known about mutual intelligibility. Even within the dialectological classification, details are disputed, such as the establishment in the 1980s of three new top-level groups: <a href="/wiki/Huizhou_Chinese" title="Huizhou Chinese">Huizhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pinghua" title="Pinghua">Pinghua</a>, despite the fact that Pinghua is itself a pair of languages and Huizhou may be half a dozen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201041–53,_55–56_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201041–53,_55–56-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYan20069–18,_61–69,_222_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYan20069–18,_61–69,_222-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like Bai, the <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a> languages are commonly thought to have split off directly from <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMei1970?_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMei1970?-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>  The evidence for this split is that all Sinitic languages apart from the Min group can be fit into the structure of the <i><a href="/wiki/Qieyun" title="Qieyun">Qieyun</a></i>, a 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Rime_dictionary" title="Rime dictionary">rime dictionary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPulleyblank19843_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPulleyblank19843-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> However, this view is not universally accepted.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Points_of_contention">Points of contention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Points of contention"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like many other language families, Sinitic languages have had problems of classification. The following are a few examples.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_China">Southern China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinitic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Southern China"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Traditionally, the <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou_dialect" title="Hangzhou dialect">lect of urban Hangzhou</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Xiang" title="New Xiang">New Xiang</a> of eastern <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> are not considered Mandarin.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas_21-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> However, linguists such as Richard VanNess Simmons and Zhou Zhenhe have observed that these two varieties possess more qualifying features of <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> languages.<sup id="cite_ref-hangzhou_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hangzhou-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> For instance, the vowels of the second division of the <i><span lang="zh-Latn">jia</span></i> (<span lang="zh">假</span>) initial is often raised and backed in Wu and Xiang, while they are not in Hangzhounese and New Xiang.
</p>
